Saunders v. State


No. 01-87-00306-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 547



March 17, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:  [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 178th District Court, Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 451,841.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the 178th District Court, Harris County (Texas), which convicted him of aggravated sexual assault and imposed a life sentence. Defendant argued that there was insufficient evidence for a conviction.


OVERVIEW: On appeal from a conviction of aggravated sexual assault, defendant contended that the evidence was insufficient to establish that complainant was placed in fear of imminent death and, therefore, that an element of the offense under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 22.012 (1988) was missing. The court recited the evidence that defendant made death threats and held a knife, and ruled that this was sufficient to support the conviction. Because all the evidence showed the aggravated nature of the assault, the court also held that there was no error in the trial court's refusal to charge the jury on the lesser offense of sexual assault. Based on the following evidence, the court held that the evidence was sufficient for the jury to have found that defendant was the perpetrator: complainant's identification of walk and manner, defendant's fingerprints at the scene, matching blood type evidence, and testimony that defendant was late for class on the day of the attack. The court found that the jury's punishment was consistent with the evidence, which showed the extreme nature of the offense, and therefore, that any error in the parole charge was harmless. The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which convicted defendant of aggravated sexual assault and imposed a life sentence, because the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction and because any error in the parole instruction to the jury was harmless.

McCullum v. State


No. 01-86-00345-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 558



March 17, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 183rd District Court of Harris County, Texas Trial Court Cause No. 439,142.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the 183rd District Court of Harris County (Texas), which found him guilty of aggravated robbery.


OVERVIEW: A complainant accused appellant of demanding his money at gunpoint. A trial court found appellant guilty of aggravated robbery and assessed punishment. The court reversed the cause with instructions that the trial court conduct a hearing to determine if there was purposeful discrimination in jury selection. The court determined that while the trial court, in its discretion, could have allowed certain questioning to show a possible bias of the complainant, it did not abuse its discretion in refusing to do so. Because appellant's counsel failed to show any other expected testimony of the two witnesses, error, if any, in refusing the request for the subpoenas was harmless constitutional error. The court found that all of the statements presented either fell within the realm of established proper jury argument, or were not so prejudicial that an instruction could not have cured any harm. The court sustained appellant's fifth point of error, asserting that the trial court committed reversible error in overruling appellant's challenge to the state's willful and systematic exclusion of blacks from the jury through peremptory challenges. Thus, the court remanded for a Batson hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a trial court's order finding appellant guilty of aggravated robbery because the trial court committed reversible error in overruling appellant's motion to challenge the state's willful and systematic exclusion of blacks from the jury by its use of peremptory challenges. The court remanded the cause with instructions that the trial court conduct a hearing to determine if there was purposeful discrimination under Batson.

 

933 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Crisman v. Cooper Industries


No. 05-87-00119-CV


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Fifth District, Dallas


748 S.W.2d 273; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 884



March 11, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] FROM A DISTRICT COURT OF DALLAS COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant executor sought review from district court of Dallas County, Texas, which dismissed appellant's action against appellee manufacturer for strict products liability and wrongful death to recover damages for the death of appellant's wife. The trial court granted appellee's motion for summary judgment and held that Florida law was applicable and that appellant's claims were barred, pursuant to Fla. Stat. ch. 95.031(b)(2) (1981).


OVERVIEW: Appellant executor initiated strict products liability and wrongful death action against appellee manufacturer to recover actual and exemplary damages for the death of his wife in a car accident in Florida. Appellee was incorporated under the laws of Ohio and conducted business and maintained its principal business office in Texas. Appellant and his wife were residents of Tennessee. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of appellee and held that Florida law was applicable and that appellant's claims were barred by the statute of repose. The court applied the "most significant relationship" test, and held that, after reviewing the qualitative nature of the contacts, Florida had the more significant relationship to facts underlying appellant's cause. The court held that the product entered the stream of commerce in Florida. The court applied Florida's statute of repose and held that, pursuant to Fla. Stat. ch. 95.031(b)(2) (1981), no action for products liability could begin within 12 years after the completed product was delivered to the original purchaser regardless of when the defect was discovered. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee manufacturer and held that Florida law was applicable and that appellant executor's claims were barred by the statute of repose. The court applied the "most significant relationship" test and held that Florida had the more significant relationship to appellant's claims and that the claims were barred by Florida's statute of repose.

Ortega v. State


Nos. 01-87-00373-CR, 01-87-00372-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 503



March 10, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 339th District Court, Harris County, Texas Trial Court Cause Nos. 467,120 and 466,417.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction in the 339th District Court of Harris County (Texas), for two separate offenses of burglary of a habitation with intent to commit theft.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his convictions for burglary of a habitation with intent to commit theft on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel and improper witness identifications, closing argument, and jury instructions. The court affirmed the convictions and held that the evidence was sufficient to convict, and appellant failed to show that, but for counsel's alleged failures, the result of the proceeding would have been different. The court held that counsel ably presented an alibi defense that created a fact question for the jury and based on the totality of appellant's representation, failure to object at trial was not unreasonable and did not mean that appellant's counsel was ineffective. The court held that appellant was not denied effective assistance of counsel when counsel failed to raise the constitutionality of a statute that had been previously held to be constitutional. The court held that there was no evidence that either a photo array or a lineup was impermissibly suggestive and any error in closing argument or jury instructions on the law of parole and good conduct time was harmless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions for two separate offenses of burglary of a habitation with intent to commit theft and held that, based on the totality of appellant's representation, appellant's counsel was not ineffective for failure to raise the constitutionality of a statute that had been previously held to be constitutional or for the failure to object at trial.

Pond v. State


No. 01-87-00274-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 508



March 10, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 178th District Court of Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 457,226


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought the review of the judgment of the 178th District Court of Harris County, Texas, which entered a jury verdict-convicting appellant of burglary of a habitation, enhanced by a prior felony conviction. The jury answered yes to a special issue inquiring whether appellant had used or exhibited a deadly weapon, namely, a firearm, during the commission of the offense or during immediate flight therefrom.


OVERVIEW: After a jury trial, appellant was convicted of burglary of a habitation. The jury also made an affirmative finding that appellant had used or exhibited a deadly weapon, namely, a firearm, during the commission of the offense or during immediate flight therefrom. Appellant fired a firearm at a police officer after being apprehended with property taken from the burglary. Appellant argued that he was denied effective trial counsel and that the evidence was insufficient to support the verdict and the affirmative finding on the firearm. The court affirmed the conviction holding that trial counsel's failure to make certain objections during trial, did not amount to ineffective assistance under the Strickland test. The court also held that letters, which appellant had written to imprisoned accomplices requesting that the accomplices deny appellant's involvement in the offense, were properly admitted as proof of appellant's guilt. Finally, the court held that the affirmative finding on the exhibiting of the deadly weapon was proper because appellant fired the firearm at the officer while attempting to run away from the consequences of his commission of the offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which convicted appellant of burglary of a habitation, enhanced by a prior felony conviction, and the jury answered "yes" to a special issue inquiring whether appellant had used or exhibited a deadly weapon, namely, a firearm, during the commission of the offense or during immediate flight therefrom; appellant was not denied effective trial counsel.

 

936 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Mercer v. Phillips Natural Gas Co.


No. 3-86-112-CV


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Third District, Austin


746 S.W.2d 933; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 718



March 9, 1988, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing overruled April 6, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF FAYETTE COUNTY, 155TH JUDICIAL DISTRICT NO. 86-321, HONORABLE OLIVER S. KITZMAN, JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought the review of the order of the 155th Judicial District Court of Fayette County (Texas), which granted appellee gas company's motion for summary judgment in appellants' suit to declare the judgment of the county court, condemning appellants' property in favor of appellee, void.


OVERVIEW: The county court entered a judgment in favor of appellee gas company ordering that appellant property owners' property be condemned and that appellee pay appellants compensation for the taking. Appellants collaterally attacked the order in the district court, but the district court affirmed the order of the county court. Appellants argued, on appeal, that the house bill, which conferred jurisdiction of the matter, concurrent with the district court, on the county court was unconstitutional and that appellee failed to pled sufficient facts in invoke the county court's special subject matter jurisdiction. The court affirmed the order of the district court holding that Tex. Const. art. § 22 (repealed), only required that the legislature, when conferring subject matter jurisdiction upon a court, conform the jurisdiction of another court only when the legislature made changes to the other court's jurisdiction, not upon a grant of concurrent jurisdiction. The court also held that appellee's pleadings were sufficient to invoke the county court's jurisdiction because the pleadings alleged the statutory basis for the suit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the district court, which granted appellee gas company's motion for summary judgment upholding the county court's judgment condemning appellant property owner's property; the statute conferring eminent domain jurisdiction upon the county court, concurrent with the district court, was not unconstitutional because the legislature was not required to conform the jurisdiction of either lower court.

Wilson v. State


No. 09-87-164 CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Ninth District, Beaumont


747 S.W.2d 490; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 768



March 9, 1988, Decided  
March 9, 1988, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE CRIMINAL DISTRICT COURT OF JEFFERSON COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction from the Criminal District Court of Jefferson County (Texas), for burglary of a building, contending that reversible error occurred when the state referred to matters outside the record, when the state admitted a pen packet into evidence, and when the parole law instruction was given to the jury.


OVERVIEW: In challenging his conviction for burglary, appellant asserted that reversible error occurred when the state's attorney referred to matters outside the record in his argument against the credibility and believability of two witnesses. The court found that the state did inject a new fact before the jury, but the court concluded that the argument did not specifically and affirmatively violate a mandatory statute, and the record showed that the new injected fact was not harmful to appellant, so there was no reversible error. The court found that when the state admitted a pen packet into evidence reflecting appellant's prior conviction for aggravated robbery, the sentence was incorrectly stated as being a minimum of two years instead of the correct minimum of five years, but that mistake did not render the conviction void, and there was no error in admitting it. The court held that the burden was on appellant to demonstrate that he was egregiously harmed by the parole law jury instruction, and appellant failed to object to the instruction or point out to the court where egregious harm could be found in the record, so there was no fundamental error. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court after applying the criteria for reversible error in criminal cases and determining, beyond a reasonable doubt, that the error appellant complained of made no contribution to his conviction or punishment.

Van Hamilton v. State


No. 01-86-00971-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 455



March 3, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 180th District Court, Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 440,914.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from the 180th District Court, Harris County, Texas, which convicted him of aggravated robbery and sentenced him to 45 years confinement after the jury found the enhancement paragraph to be true. Defendant contended that the evidence was insufficient to prove that he used a deadly weapon to sustain the aggravated robbery conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated robbery and was sentenced to 45 years confinement. Defendant contended that the evidence was insufficient to prove that the baseball bat he used during the robbery was a deadly weapon, one of the elements of aggravated robbery, pursuant to Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 29.03 (1974). The court held that, pursuant to Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 1.07(1), the test for determining what was a deadly weapon was either "objective" or "subjective:" a firearm or anything manifestly designed, made, or adapted for the purpose of inflicting death or serious bodily injury; or anything in the manner of its use or intended use that was capable of causing death or serious bodily injury. The court held that the evidence was sufficient for the jury to find that the bat was a deadly weapon. The complainant testified that he thought defendant was going to strike him with the bat, and a police officer testified that the bat defendant used was capable of causing serious bodily injury or death. The court held that the statute addressing the affirmative finding that defendant used a deadly weapon was not unconstitutionally vague. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of aggravated robbery and sentenced him to 45 years confinement. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to prove that the bat defendant used during the robbery was a deadly weapon, one of the elements of aggravated robbery, because complainant thought defendant was going to strike him with the bat and the bat was capable of causing serious bodily injury/death.

 

939 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Rodriguez v. State


Nos. 01-87-00296-CR, 01-87-00297-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


746 S.W.2d 927; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 466



March 3, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Appeal from the 179th District Court of Harris County, Texas Trial Court Cause Nos. 451,838 & 453,272.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction from the 179th District Court of Harris County (Texas), for two separate offenses of aggravated kidnapping, after a joint jury trial with two co-defendants. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in instructing the jury and in requiring him to submit a redacted statement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who was convicted of aggravated kidnapping, admitted that he did not request an instruction on mitigation or object to the charge that was given, and the court found that the evidence did not support the issue of voluntary release within the statutory meaning, only intent to release, since there was still the possibility that the complainants could be harmed in a rescue action. In light of the evidence, the totality of the proceedings, and the severity of the offense, the court found that appellant did not suffer any egregious harm from the parole law jury charge, despite assessment of the maximum punishment. Appellant asserted that the trial court erred in refusing to charge the jury on the lesser included offense of false imprisonment. However, the court held that appellant's restraint of the complainants was in a place where he knew that they were unlikely to be found, so the jury could not have reasonably concluded that he was guilty only of false imprisonment. Appellant failed to preserve any error in forcing him to redact his written statement for appeal. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed. Whether or not appellant had any intent to release the complainants, there was no voluntary release within the statutory meaning. The parole law jury charge did not submit appellant to any egregious harm. No jury could have concluded that appellant was guilty only of false imprisonment.

 

940 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Musick v. Railroad Com. of Texas


No. 3-87-132-CV


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Third District, Austin


747 S.W.2d 892; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 823; 104 Oil & Gas Rep. 148



February 24, 1988, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF TRAVIS COUNTY, 250TH JUDICIAL DISTRICT NO. 400,454, HONORABLE HARLEY CLARK, JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, drilling permit applicants, sought review of a decision by the District Court of Travis County, 250th Judicial District (Texas), which refused to reverse the final order of appellee Texas Railroad Commission in an application for a permit to drill the first well on a small tract of land acquired by appellants.


OVERVIEW: Appellant land lessee applied to appellee Texas Railroad Commission for a permit to drill and complete a well in a natural-gas field. There were several wells that surrounded the small tract. Appellee's spacing rules stated that the minimum distance between a well and the nearest property or lease line was 330 feet, but they allowed an exception when it was necessary to prevent waste or confiscation of the property. The distance between appellant's proposed well to the boundaries of her small tract was only 20 feet in either direction. Appellee determined that appellant was not entitled to the exception because she had not shown that it was necessary to prevent confiscation of any right she had acquired. The trial court refused to reverse appellee's final order. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. Appellant did not show that her needs fit under the exception. Appellant was accorded a fair opportunity with producers of surrounding tracts within the drainage area to recover her fair share of natural gas beneath her tract. Appellant could not acquire from her lessor a right to a well permit to prevent confiscation when the lessor himself had none.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of appellee Texas Railroad Commission. The exception to the spacing rule did not apply to appellant because she did not show that it was necessary to prevent waste or confiscation of the property. The court further acknowledged that appellant had no better right to a well permit than the lessor previously had.

 

941 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Briggs v. State


No. 05-85-00894-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Fifth District, Dallas


746 S.W.2d 331; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 524



February 19, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] FROM A DISTRICT COURT OF DALLAS COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After remand from the Court of Criminal Appeals (Texas), appellant sought review of the judgment of a Texas trial court, which convicted him of the unlawful possession of a prohibited weapon, a chemical dispensing device, in violation of Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 46.06(a)(7).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of unlawful possession of a prohibited weapon, a chemical dispensing device, in violation of Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 46.06(a)(7). The appellate court reversed the finding of the court that § 46.06(a)(7) was unconstitutionally vague, and remanded for the court to address appellant's remaining points of error. On appeal, the court affirmed, and held that the evidence admitted at trial was sufficient to support appellant's conviction. The record showed that the device appellant possessed was a tear gas grenade manufactured for the U. S. armed forces for the purpose of riot control. In addition, the grenade was not sold commercially. The court also held that the indictment was not void because the state failed to plead that what appellant possessed was a chemical dispensing devise other than one sold commercially for personal protection. Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 2.02 required only that the indictment negate express exceptions so labeled in the code, not implied exceptions. Finally, the court held that appellant had no standing to raise the vagueness issue because his conduct fell within the core of proscribed conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction. The court held that the evidence admitted at trial was sufficient to support appellant's conviction for unlawful possession of a prohibited weapon. The court also held that the indictment against appellant was not void because it failed to negate implied exceptions to the applicable statute, and that appellant had no standing to assert that the statute was constitutionally vague.

Gilliam v. State


No. 11-87-048-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Eleventh District, Eastland


746 S.W.2d 323; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 328



February 18, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from Nolan County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of a Nolan County (Texas) trial court, which convicted him of burglary, enhanced his punishment based on 2 prior felonies, and sentenced him to 50 years.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, convicted of burglary for entering a boathouse and taking personal property therein without consent of the owner, sought relief from the conviction. The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of appellant because the boathouse was a building or enclosed structure, under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 30. 01(2), as it was intended for use or occupation as a habitation or for some purpose of trade, manufacture, ornament or use. Appellant's argument that evidence was insufficient to show that the boathouse was a building under the statute failed. Though the boathouse was on the surface of a lake and could be entered by swimming under the structure, it was an enclosed structure to secure the owner's boat and property and used for storage. The doors to the boathouse closed and locked. The court found no merit in appellant's position that it was error not to instruct the jury to disregard appellant's parole for past convictions. The trial court in no way mentioned parole to the jury. The court found appellant's indictment did not fail for lack of listing property taken or the owner of the stolen property and held that appellant's indictment gave adequate notice of the charges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of appellant by the trial court because the boathouse appellant entered was a building as defined in the burglary statute, it was not error for the trial court to disregard jury instructions concerning appellant's past parole, and appellant's indictment gave adequate notice of the charges against him even though it failed to list items taken and name of the property owner.

Edmondson v. State


No. C14-87-108-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Fourteenth District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 373



February 18, 1988, Decided and Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Appeal from the 338th District Court of Harris County, Cause No. 465,798.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the denial of her writ of habeas corpus by the 338th District Court of Harris County (Texas), which found that appellee state's extradition documents were regular and that appellant did not assert any facts to disprove appellee's prima facie case.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a resident of Texas, was sought for extradition by Louisiana for the offense of simple burglary. Appellant sought a writ of habeas corpus to challenge the validity of the extradition statute and the Louisiana documents. The court denied the writ and held that the record clearly showed that appellant's counsel was permitted to argue the facts supporting her contentions. The amended petition, which the trial court denied leave to file, was not in the record, thus, the court could not determine if the requirements of Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 11.14 (1977) were met. The court refused to consider, on appeal, bond hearing issues that were not presented to the trial court. The Louisiana Governor's Warrant was a self-authenticating public document that was regular on its face and met all the requirements of Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 51.13, § 3 (1979) and was properly admitted. Appellant presented nothing for review to say that the extradition statute was unconstitutional, or that being held more than 90 days was error. Article 51.13 conformed to U.S. Const. amends. IV and XIV, and appellant had no right to be arraigned prior to extradition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which denied appellant a writ of habeas corpus, and held that appellant's counsel was permitted to argue appellant's contentions, that the extradition documents were in order and meet all Texas requirements, and that appellant had no right to be arraigned prior to extradition. On all other points of error, appellant presented nothing for review by the appellate court.

 

944 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Perez v. State


No. 01-87-00152-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 268



February 11, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 228th District Court of Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 459,721.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence for possession with the intent to deliver at least 400 grams of cocaine from the 288th District Court, Harris County, Texas.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant possession with the intent to deliver at least 400 grams of cocaine. He was sentenced to 17 years in prison and to pay a fine. Defendant contended his conviction and sentence should be overturned due to instructional error by the court and improper prosecutorial argument. The court rejected both contentions and affirmed. It was not automatically reversible error for the trial court to instruct the jury on the law of parole and good time conduct under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07 (4) (1988). Because defendant did not object to the lower court's charge, defendant had to show that the error was so egregious and created such harm that he did not receive a fair and impartial trial, in light of the entire jury charge, the voir dire, the evidence of guilt, the argument of counsel, the actual punishment assessed, and any other relevant information. Defendant did not sustain this burden. Further, the prosecutor's reference, during sentencing argument, to matters of public knowledge and to evidence outside the record merited no relief on appeal because the statements were not so manifestly improper as to constitute reversible error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, sentence, and fine for possession with the intent to deliver at least 400 grams of cocaine because neither a jury instruction on parole law and good time conduct nor allegedly improper prosecutorial sentencing argument constituted reversible error.

Daniels v. State


No. 01-87-00185-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 287



February 11, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 178th District Court of Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 465,780.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the 178th District Court of Harris County, Texas, which convicted him of injury to a child and assessed punishment at 99 years confinement.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was associated with the 13 year-old victim. While the victim was visiting defendant, defendant asked the victim if she would have sex with him. When she declined, defendant began to strangle her. When the victim attempted to escape, defendant stabbed her three times in the back, piercing both lungs, and locked her in the attic. The trial court convicted defendant of injury to a child. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the police officer's testimony that the victim, as she was drifting in and out of consciousness and incoherent, identified defendant as the one who stabbed her was properly admitted as an excited utterance exception to the hearsay rule. The court also found that the trial court's charge on parole law pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4 (1987), did not deny defendant a fair trial because defendant did not object to the charge at trial and the record did not indicate that the parole charge influenced the jury's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the identification of defendant as the assailant by the victim, who was drifting in and out of consciousness, was an excited utterance exception to the hearsay rule. The trial court's charge on parole law did not deny defendant a fair and impartial trial given there was no indication that the jury's decision was influenced by it.

 

946 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


House v. State


No. 01-87-00567-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 288



February 11, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 262nd District Court, Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 469022.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the 262nd District Court, Harris County, Texas, in which a jury found him guilty of burglary of a motor vehicle with intent to commit theft, determined two enhancement paragraphs to be true, and assessed punishment at 50 years' confinement. Defendant contended that reversible error occurred when the trial court instructed the jury, under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4 (1988).


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of burglary of a motor vehicle with intent to commit theft, determined two enhancement paragraphs to be true, and assessed punishment at 50 years' confinement. Defendant appealed, challenging the constitutionality of Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4 (1988), pursuant to which the trial court instructed the jury on the law of parole and good conduct. Defendant made no objection to the instruction at trial. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding although art. 37.07, § 4 had recently been declared unconstitutional, any error in giving an instruction pursuant to the statute did not warrant automatic reversal. Because defendant did not object at trial, reversal was required only if the error was so egregious and created such harm that defendant was denied a fair and impartial trial. The actual degree of harm had to be determined in light of the entire jury charge, the state of the evidence, the argument of counsel, and any other relevant information contained in the record. Finding no egregious harm resulting from the instruction, the court overruled defendant's points of error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of burglary of a motor vehicle with intent to commit theft, holding that although the instruction on the law of parole and good conduct had recently been held unconstitutional in another case, where defendant did not object at trial, no reversal was required where the error was not so egregious and did not create such harm as to deny a fair and impartial trial.

Dougherty v. State


No. 07-87-0079-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Seventh District, Amarillo


745 S.W.2d 107; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 239



February 10, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Motion for Rehearing Overruled March 8, 1988.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction by a Texas court of the misdemeanor offense of driving while intoxicated.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of the misdemeanor of driving while intoxicated. He appealed on the ground that his motion to dismiss should have been granted because the arresting officer was not in proper uniform, pursuant to Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 6701d-9 (1977), and could therefore not effect a proper arrest. The court affirmed the conviction, and held that art. 6701d-9 was unconstitutional, following a 1931 court of criminal appeals ruling on a nearly identical statute, and citing Tex. Const. art. 1, § 28, which provided that no power of suspending laws in the state would be exercised except by the legislature. The court reasoned that the arresting officer could not be vested with the option to either enforce the law or, discarding his uniform, suspend it as he pleased.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for driving while intoxicated even though the arresting officer was not in proper uniform as required by Texas law, because the law was unconstitutional.

 

948 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


$2067 in U.S. Currency, 3 Handguns & 51 Capsules v. State


No. 2-87-062-CV


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Second District, Fort Worth


745 S.W.2d 109; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 452



February 10, 1988, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of the judgment of a Texas trial court, which ordered the forfeiture of $ 2067 in U.S. currency, 3 handguns, and 51 capsules containing a controlled substance to the State of Texas pursuant to Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4476-15, § 5.03 (1988).


OVERVIEW: The lower court ordered appellant property owner to forfeit $ 2067 in U.S. currency, 3 handguns, and 51 capsules containing a controlled substance to the State of Texas pursuant to Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4476-15, § 5.03 (1988). On appeal, the court reversed and held that the evidence was insufficient to support the forfeiture. The court held that the state's evidence that the property was derived from the sale or distribution of drugs was unpersuasive where the arresting officer admitted he never personally observed appellant in any drug transaction and did not have personal knowledge that the money was the proceeds of a drug transaction. The court held that it was just as likely that the manner in which the drugs were packaged indicated they were recently purchased rather than prepared for sale. The fact that the forfeited property was found at, or near, the controlled substance did not establish the required nexus. The court also held that the trial court erred in failing to suppress the evidence because the arresting officer was permitted only to handle the bags in appellant's purse in a manner to determine whether any of them contained a weapon.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment below, which ordered appellant property owner to forfeit money, 3 handguns, and capsules containing a controlled substance, holding that the evidence was insufficient to support the forfeiture because it did not demonstrate that the property was derived from the sale or distribution of drugs. The court also held that the trial court erred in failing to suppress the illegally obtained evidence.

Hassell v. State


No. 01-87-00230-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 198



February 4, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the 18th District Court Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 454,806.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the 18th District Court Harris County (Texas), which convicted him of arson and sentenced him to five years confinement, after a jury found that he had set his estranged wife's house on fire.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted, at a jury trial, of arson after he was charged with setting fire to his estranged wife's house. Appellant sought review of his conviction and alleged that he failed to receive reasonably effective assistance of counsel at trial and that the parole law charge mandated by Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4, was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the conviction and held that, although certain items of evidence that were admitted, without objection, were inadmissible hearsay and prejudicial to appellant's case, appellant was not denied effective assistance of counsel because many of the hearsay statements were cumulative and the court did not find that appellant's counsel's trial strategy lacked any plausible basis. Furthermore, the court found that even though the parole instruction had been held unconstitutional, the giving of that instruction was not reversible error because there was no objection to the instruction and no indication that egregious harm resulted from the instruction on parole law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision, convicting appellant of arson, because the court found that appellant had not been denied the effective assistance of counsel and because the giving of the parole instruction did not create egregious harm even though it was unconstitutional.

Click v. State


No. 13-87-104-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Thirteenth District, Corpus Christi


745 S.W.2d 480; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 203



February 4, 1988, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Texas trial court, which convicted appellant of driving while intoxicated, based on a breath intoxilyzer test appellant submitted to after being arrested.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was arrested for driving while intoxicated in a county of less than 25,000 people, and later convicted for the same, based on a breath intoxilyzer test. Appellant sought review of the conviction challenging that it should be reversed because his arresting officer failed to make a videotape recording of him in violation of Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 67011-1, § 24. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court and held that the arresting officer did not violate Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 67011-1, § 24, because the statute required the mandatory use of video recording equipment in all DWI cases in counties with populations of 25,000 or more. The court also held that the statute did not apply to a county of less than 25,000 people even if the county owned video recording equipment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that the arresting officer was under no statutory duty to make a videotape of appellant while he was intoxicated, and that Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art 67011-1, § 24 was constitutional as applied only to counties with populations greater than 25,000.

 

951 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Morehead v. State


No. 05-87-00038-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Fifth District, Dallas


746 S.W.2d 830; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 723



February 2, 1988, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's Motion for Rehearing on Petition for Discretionary Review Denied March 6, 1991. State's Motion for Rehearing on Petition for Discretionary Review Denied March 6, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: FROM A COUNTY COURT OF DALLAS COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment rendered by the County Court of Dallas County (Texas) that convicted appellant of disrupting a lawful meeting in violation of Tex. Penal Code § 42.05.


OVERVIEW: Appellant disrupted a public meeting of a popular national speaker by yelling at the speaker while approaching the podium. After a struggle with police officers, appellant was escorted out of the convention center. Witnesses testified that the entire disturbance lasted from thirty seconds to two minutes. Appellant was convicted of disrupting a lawful meeting in violation of Tex. Penal Code § 42.05. Appellant sought review of the judgment on several grounds. Appellant contended that § 42.05 was unconstitutional in that it was facially overbroad, overbroad as applied, and vague therefore violating U.S. Const. amend I and XIV and Tex. Const. art. I, § 8. The court concluded that § 42.05 on its face applied to every person who intentionally disrupted any meeting through physical actions or verbal utterance. Although the court found that the statute was unconstitutionally broad, under a narrowing doctrine, the court found that the manner of expression was incompatible with the normal activity of a particular place at a particular time. The court affirmed the judgment. The court found that the statute was constitutional in all respects and appellant's other contentions was overruled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that convicted appellant for disrupting a lawful meeting because the statute under which appellant was convicted was constitutional and appellant's other contentions was overruled.

 

952 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Sunday v. State


No. 09-86-124 CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Ninth District, Beaumont


745 S.W.2d 436; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 495



January 27, 1988, Decided  
January 27, 1988, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the trial court convicting him of murder and use of a deadly weapon in the course and commission of the offense.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of murder and the use of a deadly weapon in the course and commission of the offense and sentenced to 15 years. Appellant sought review, and the court reversed, finding that the record clearly showed that one juror consistently exhibited a prejudice against the law which set the minimum punishment for the offense of murder at five years' probation, and her statements revealed that she would only consider such punishment appropriate in a mercy killing. The court found that this was a prejudice against the minimum legal punishment for which a challenge for cause may be made under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 35.16(c) (1966). The trial court erred in permitting the state to attack appellant's trial attorney during closing argument because the attacks struck at the core of appellant's exculpatory story and violated appellant's right to counsel under U.S. Const. amend. VI. The court found that the trial court did not err in instructing the jury as to the law of parole and good time credit as mandated by Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07 § 4 (Supp. 1987), because both the statute and instruction were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded appellant's conviction for murder and use of a deadly weapon because a juror exhibited a prejudice against the law and was subject to challenge for cause and because state's argument attacking defense counsel was inflammatory and violated right to counsel.

 

953 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Whitehead v. American Industrial Transp., Inc.


No. 9554


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Sixth District, Texarkana


746 S.W.2d 273; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 107



January 26, 1988, Decided  
January 26, 1988, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from the 4th Judicial District Court of Rusk County, Texas.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of judgment of the 4th Judicial District Court, Rusk County (Texas), granting summary judgment for appellee employer in appellant's wrongful death action based on gross negligence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother brought suit against appellee employer for the wrongful death of her son under Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code Ann. §§ 71.001 et seq. (1986). The trial court granted summary judgment on the ground that the suit was barred by the Workers' Compensation Act, Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art 8306, § 3 (1967 & Supp. 1988). The court affirmed, finding that appellant failed to show by summary judgment evidence that the § 3 provision, which waived the right to common law or statutory actions against an employer, was unconstitutional as it applied to her son, a minor. The court found that due process under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Tex. Const. art. I, § 13 required a determination of whether a particular individual was denied an opportunity to choose his legal remedy at a meaningful time and in a meaningful way, which depended on that person's age, experience, maturity, and judgment. The court found that appellant failed to allege that her son personally did not have sufficient capacity or knowledge to make a meaningful decision concerning his workers' compensation benefits, and thus that the summary judgment was properly granted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed grant of summary judgment in wrongful death action for appellee employer because appellant mother failed to show by summary judgment evidence that her son lacked sufficient capacity to waive legal remedy concerning worker's compensation benefits.

 

954 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Schin v. State


No. 05-87-00340-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Fifth District, Dallas


744 S.W.2d 370; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 300



January 22, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] FROM A DISTRICT COURT OF DALLAS COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction from a District Court Of Dallas County (Texas), on the grounds that the trial court erred in denying appellant's motions to dismiss based on the speedy disposition provision of the Speedy Trial Act, Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 32A.02, § 1(1), and the Interstate Agreement on Detainers Act, Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 51.14.


OVERVIEW: While in jail in Arizona in 1985, appellant was served with an outstanding Texas warrant and federal detainer from Kansas. Appellant wrote to the Texas authorities and requested a speedy trial and disposition of all charges. After his transfer from Arizona to Kansas, appellant was moved to Texas on the outstanding warrant. Appellant stood trial in February, 1987. Appellant filed motions to dismiss the charges based on violations of the Speedy Trial Act, Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 32A.02, § 1(1), and the Interstate Agreement of Detainers Act, Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 51.14, which were denied. Appellant was convicted of theft of property pursuant to a plea bargain agreement. Appellant sought review of the denial of his motions, and the court affirmed and held that the Speedy Trial Act was void, and that Arizona's procedural errors caused the delay in appellant being sent to Texas and did not entitle appellant to a dismissal, and that excluding the excusable delays of incarceration in Kansas and another Texas county, appellant was actually brought to trial within the 180-day limitation of the Interstate Agreement of Detainers Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant's motions and held that appellant was not entitled to a dismissal of the charges because the Speedy Trial Act was void, the delay in being sent to Texas was caused by Arizona's procedural errors, and that excluding the excusable delays of incarceration in Kansas and another Texas county, appellant was actually brought to trial within the 180-day limitation of the Interstate Agreement of Detainers Act.

 

955 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


State Bank of Omaha v. Means


No. 9563


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Sixth District, Texarkana


746 S.W.2d 269; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 54



January 20, 1988, Decided  
January 20, 1988, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from the 76th Judicial District Court of Titus County, Texas


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bank sought review of the take nothing judgment from the 76th Judicial District Court, Titus County, Texas, which found against appellant in appellant's action against appellee city for damages resulting from appellee's demolition of two portable buildings on which appellant had a lien.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bank sued appellee city for damages resulting from appellee's demolition of two buildings on which appellant had a lien. A jury found that the buildings constituted a nuisance and determined their value. The lower court refused to allow appellant a recovery and entered a take nothing judgment. Appellant argued that it should have recovered the market value of the buildings. Appellant also claimed that the ordinance that authorized the demolition of the buildings was unconstitutional because it did not provide for notice to those holding liens on the property to be destroyed. The court agreed that the ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to appellant because it provided for notice and an opportunity of hearing to the property owners, but it did not provide for any notice to appellant. The court affirmed the take-nothing judgment. To recover the damages resulting from the demolition of the buildings, the court found that appellant had the burden to secure a finding on the amount necessary to bring the buildings into compliance. Having failed to request an issue on that ground of recovery, appellant waived it and there was no basis on which to award damages.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the take nothing judgment entered against appellant bank because even though appellee city's ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to appellant, appellant failed to request and obtain jury issues necessary to establish a proper recovery.

 

956 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Chenault v. Bexar County


No. 04-87-00300-CV


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Fourth District, San Antonio


747 S.W.2d 400; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 799



January 20, 1988, Decided  
January 20, 1988, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the 73rd District Court of Bexar County, Trial Court Nos. 86-CI-20054 & 86-CI-23203, Honorable Joe E. Kelly, Judge Presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public officials challenged the judgment of the 73rd District Court of Bexar County (Texas), which upheld the order of appellee county that abolished three Justice of the Peace positions in the county.


OVERVIEW: Appellee county issued an order abolishing three Justice of the Peace positions in the county. Appellant public officials had been elected as Justices of the Peace and filed suits alleging that the order was void because it violated the Tex. Const. art. XVI, § 65. The trial court upheld the order and denied appellants all relief sought by them. After review, this court concluded that the order did not conflict with the provision of the Texas Constitution, enabling legislation was not required in order for appellee to take the action, and the order was not arbitrary and capricious. The court held that appellee did not abuse its discretion by passing the order, and appellee was not required to accept appellants' oath of office and issue a certificate of election since the office had already been lawfully abolished; there was no constitutional or statutory limit on when the order had to become effective. The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that upheld the order of appellee county abolishing three Justice of the Peace positions was affirmed because the order did not violate provisions of the Texas Constitution. Appellee did not abuse its discretion by passing the order, and appellee was not required to accept the oath of office of appellants, public officials, and issue a certificate of election since the office had already been lawfully abolished.

Adams v. Texas State Bd. of Chiropractic Examiners


No. 3-87-044-CV


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Third District, Austin


744 S.W.2d 648; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 305



January 13, 1988, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF TRAVIS COUNTY, 200TH JUDICIAL DISTRICT, NO. 362,146, HONORABLE PAUL R. DAVIS, JR., JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant chiropractor disputed a decision of the 200th District Court of Travis County (Texas), which upheld the order of appellee Texas State Board of Chiropractic Examiners revoking appellant's license to practice chiropractic. He argued that he was denied his right to a jury trial, he was improperly required to delete causes of action from his complaint, the order was invalid, appellee exceeded its authority, and he was denied due process.


OVERVIEW: Appellant chiropractor was placed on probation by appellee Texas State Board of Chiropractic Examiners. Based on a second incident and four other grounds, appellee revoked appellant's license. The district court upheld the order. On appeal, the court held that Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4512b, § 14(d) did not provide "de novo" review, so it required a judge-only trial. 1981 Tex. Gen. Laws 781, §1 constitutionally rescinded any right to jury trial. Tex. Const. art. I, § 15 was limited to rights existing in 1876. Even though it was a property right, the license was subject to statutory revocation. Special circumstances made a jury trial inappropriate. The district court had not abused its discretion in deleting causes of action. Appellant did not challenge the probation, so revocation based on its violation validated the revocation. Appellee had the authority to revoke the license. The 1983 amendment of Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4512b, §14a did not indicate that the authority had not existed before the amendment, which was constitutionally valid even though it had no title. Appellant was not denied due process. He was not prejudiced by any findings. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. The administrative procedure act constitutionally could provide for a judge-only trial. The district court did not abuse its discretion in deleting causes of action. Revocation based on violation of a probation order validated the revocation order. Appellee State Board of Chiropractic Examiners had authority to revoke the license. Appellant chiropractor was not denied due process, nor was he prejudiced by improper findings.

 

958 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Beasley v. State


No. 01-86-00952-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, FIRST DISTRICT, HOUSTON


745 S.W.2d 406; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 15



January 7, 1988, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the trial court (Texas), which convicted him of burglary of a motor vehicle. He argued that it was error to refuse to permit him to enter a plea on double jeopardy grounds, that the fruits of an inventory search of his automobile should have been suppressed, that a jury instruction was based on an unconstitutional statute and was misleading, and that the evidence was insufficient to show an intent to commit theft.


OVERVIEW: Defendant disputed a conviction for burglary of a motor vehicle. In an earlier trial on the charge, he had moved for a mistrial based on the state's failure to make disclosures. The court pointed out that defendant did not have a general right to discover evidence from the state. The court held that the trial court had not erred in refusing to allow defendant's plea in bar, because there was no evidence that the prosecutor had intentionally provoked defendant's motion for a mistrial. The court reviewed the circumstances surrounding defendant's arrest, and held that entry into his automobile was reasonable, and that an inventory was authorized to protect the vehicle. Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4 (Supp. 1987) was unconstitutional. However, any error in the jury charge was harmless. There was no evidence that the instruction denied defendant a fair trial by influencing the jury to enhance his punishment. The instruction was not misleading. Viewing the evidence in the light most favorable to the verdict, defendant's entry into the vehicle was proved by testimony and his intent to commit theft could be inferred from the circumstances. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. The trial court did not err in refusing to allow the plea in bar. There was no evidence that the prosecutor intentionally provoked defendant's motion for a mistrial. Entry into his automobile was reasonable, and an inventory was done to protect it. The statute was unconstitutional, but any error in the charge was harmless. The instruction was not misleading. Intent to commit theft could be inferred from the circumstances.

Thibeaux v. State


No. 01-87-00084-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 29



January 7, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 337th District Court, Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 452,492.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a jury verdict from the 337th District Court, Harris County (Texas), convicting him of possession of a controlled substance, to wit, cocaine weighing less than 28 grams.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his jury conviction for possessing a controlled substance, namely, cocaine weighing less than 28 grams. Defendant argued that the prosecutor's closing argument at the punishment phase of trial implied that defendant was guilty of the more serious crime of delivery of a controlled substance. On appeal, the court held that the prosecutor's argument was not tantamount to asking the jury to assess additional punishment for an act for which defendant was neither indicted nor tried. Rather, the prosecutor was attempting to persuade the jury to assess a more severe punishment within the sentencing range permitted for the crime of possessing cocaine. The trial court's instructing on the law of parole and good time as required by Tex. Crim. P. Code Ann. art. 37.07, §4 (1987) was not unconstitutional and was, at most, harmless error because defendant failed to contemporaneously object. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of a controlled substance, finding that the prosecutor's sentencing argument was not the equivalent of asking the jury to convict for a more serious crime, delivery of a controlled substance, with which defendant was not charged but, rather, urged the jury to impose the longest sentence permitted under the range of sentences for possession.

 

960 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Medina v. State


Nos. 01-87-0060-CR, 01-87-00061-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 33



January 7, 1988, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 339th District Court, Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause Nos. 434,035 and 434,036.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the 339th District Court, Harris County (Texas) where he was found guilty by a jury of murder and of attempted murder. Two enhancement paragraphs were found true. Punishment was assessed at 35 years confinement in each case. Defendant claimed error in the denial of his motion for mistrial, as a juror saw him handcuffed, and in a jury instruction on parole under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from his conviction by a jury of murder and of attempted murder. He had moved for a mistrial after a juror saw him in handcuffs while being transported to the courtroom and had objected to a jury instruction on parole under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4. He urged that it was error to deny his motion and overrule his objection to the instruction. The court affirmed defendant's conviction where although the instruction under art. 37.07, § 4 was error, as the statute had been found unconstitutional, the error was not automatically reversible. The court stated a showing of harm was required for reversal and found the instruction harmless where, in light of the entire jury charge, the evidence, and the jury argument, there was no evidence that the instruction influenced the jury to enhance punishment. The court further found that although his constitutional presumption of innocence may have been infringed by a juror seeing him handcuffed, an inadvertent glimpse of him by a juror was not necessarily so prejudicial as to require reversal of his conviction, where as such prejudice could have been cured by a jury instruction which he had failed to request.


OUTCOME: The judgment of defendant's conviction of murder and attempted murder was affirmed where, although the jury instruction based on the unconstitutional parole statute was error, the instruction was harmless as there was no evidence that the parole charge influenced the jury to enhance defendant's punishment, and he had failed to request a curative instruction as to any infringement of the presumption of innocence when a juror saw him handcuffed.

 

961 of 1887 DOCUMENTS


Shelby v. State


No. 05-86-00109-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Fifth District, Dallas


764 S.W.2d 346; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 3393



January 6, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] FROM A DISTRICT COURT OF DALLAS COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the Texas Court of Criminal Appeals, wherein defendant's petition for discretionary review was granted and the court's judgment, that Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4 was constitutional, was vacated, the court was to conduct a harm analysis under Tex. R. App. P. 81(b)(2) of the error in an instruction on art. 37.07, § 4 when the court of criminal appeals held in another case that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had petitioned for discretionary review of the court's judgment rejecting defendant's argument that the trial court erred in instructing the jury on the parole law under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.07, § 4 as the statute was unconstitutional. The Texas Criminal Court of Appeals, having ruled in another case that art. 37.07, § 4 was unconstitutional, therefore granted defendant's petition, vacated the court's judgment, and remanded for a harm analysis under Tex. R. App. P. 81(b)(2) of the error from the jury instruction. In affirming the judgment of defendant's conviction of murder, the court held that, beyond a reasonable doubt, the error in giving the jury instruction under art. 37.07, § 4 was harmless where it made no contribution to the punishment assessed. The court based its holding on its findings as to the lack of emphasis on the parole statute by the parties throughout the course of the trial, coupled with the particular facts of the offense committed by defendant, where there was evidence that he had shot the victim in the back while the victim was fleeing from him and that the victim may have been unarmed, and defendant's prior criminal record.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction of murder was affirmed where, although the jury instruction based on an unconstitutional parole statute was error, the court found beyond a reasonable doubt that error was harmless, where the instruction made no contribution to the punishment assessed given the lack of emphasis on the statute by the parties during the trial, the particular facts of the offense committed by defendant, and his prior criminal record.

Massie v. State


No. 05-87-00427-CR


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, Fifth District, Dallas


744 S.W.2d 314; 1988 Tex. App. LEXIS 298



January 5, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] FROM A COUNTY COURT OF DALLAS COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a conviction from the County Court of Dallas County (Texas), for driving while intoxicated, pursuant to Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 6701(1). Defendant challenged the constitutionality and construction of the statute, asserted that the evidence was admitted in violation of his Miranda rights and right to silence, and complained that the trial court failed to properly instruct the jury.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with driving while intoxicated, pursuant to Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 6701(1). The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague and found that the state proved that defendant drove a motor vehicle while intoxicated and that he drove it in a public place. The court determined that questions such as name, address, and telephone number were not interrogation under Miranda and that defendant's U.S. Const. amend. V rights were not violated when police asked him questions which were not reasonably likely to elicit an incriminating response. The court held that the statute allowed evidence that defendant refused to take the breathalyzer test to be admitted as evidence of guilt, and held that the trial court properly refused to give an instruction that defendant's refusal to take the test was not to be considered an admission of guilt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for driving while intoxicated. The court held that the statute was constitutional and properly constructed, and that defendant's Fifth Amendment rights were not violated where routine questions were asked which were not reasonably likely to elicit an incriminating response. The court further affirmed the trial court's jury instructions as proper.

Muldrew v. State


No. 01-86-00153-CR.


COURT OF APPEALS OF TEXAS, First District, Houston


1987 Tex. App. LEXIS 9126



December 31, 1987, Decided


NOTICE:   [*1] PURSUANT TO RULE 90(i) OF THE TEXAS RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE, UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS SHALL NOT BE CITED AS AUTHORITY BY COUNSEL OR BY A COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Appeal from the 228th District Court of Harris County, Texas, Trial Court Cause No. 435,863.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the 228th District Court, Harris County (Texas) which, upon a jury verdict, entered a conviction against him for murder. Defendant argued in two points of error that the lower court's parole charge was erroneously given and that his trial lawyer was ineffective.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested without warrant after several witnesses identified him to police as the one who robbed and killed a taxi cab driver. A jury later found defendant guilty of murder. Defendant contended that the lower court's parole charge was erroneously given, however, defendant did not object to such charge at trial. The court held that where the only obvious purpose for defendant's shooting of an unresisting victim to a completed robbery was to eliminate her as a witness, such facts were more likely than the lower court's error in giving the parole instruction to have brought about a verdict of life imprisonment; therefore, the instruction was not so egregious and harmful as to deprive defendant of a fair and impartial punishment hearing. Furthermore, the court held that where no objection was made as to the admissibility of certain evidence resulting from defendant's arrest and defendant alleged ineffective counsel on appeal, defendant had the burden to establish that his arrest did not comply with any exception allowing a warrantless search and defendant failed to meet such burden. As such, the court affirmed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder, holding that where the only obvious purpose for defendant's shooting of an unresisting victim to a completed robbery was to eliminate her as a witness, such facts were more likely than the trial court's improper parole instruction to have brought about a life sentence; as such, the instruction was not so egregious and harmful as to deprive defendant of a fair and impartial punishment hearing.

Martinez v. State

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner challenged his two convictions for aggravated kidnapping entered in the 23rd District Court Brazoria County (Texas).


OVERVIEW: Appellant prisoner was convicted of two causes of aggravated kidnapping following an incident at the prison where he was incarcerated. On appeal, the court affirmed appellant's convictions. The court held that the trial court did not err in denying appellant's request for a charge on a lesser included offense of kidnapping because the evidence did not show that appellant was guilty only of the lesser offense, that the trial court's single jury instruction applied to appellant and to the other defendant tried with him was neither confusing or misleading, that appellant's threatening statement made to the warden the day following the offense was admissible to a material issue in the case, and that, while the jury instruction regarding the existence of parole laws and how they work was unconstitutional, informing the jury did not cause reversible error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of appellant prisoner of the two causes of aggravated kidnapping.
